Based on the input 1, I will revise the draft report.

**Revised Report:**

**Title:** The Impact of Artificial Intelligence on Healthcare: Opportunities and Challenges

**Executive Summary:**

Artificial Intelligence (AI) is transforming the healthcare industry by improving diagnosis, treatment, and patient outcomes. This report highlights the benefits and challenges associated with AI adoption in healthcare, including improved diagnosis, personalized medicine, predictive analytics, and enhanced patient engagement. However, it also discusses the concerns surrounding AI adoption, such as data quality and bias, regulatory frameworks, and cybersecurity risks.

**Introduction:**

The use of Artificial Intelligence (AI) in healthcare is becoming increasingly prevalent due to its potential to improve diagnosis, treatment, and patient outcomes. AI algorithms can analyze large amounts of medical data, including images and lab results, to help doctors make more accurate diagnoses. Additionally, AI-powered predictive models can identify high-risk patients and predict the likelihood of readmission or complications, allowing for targeted interventions.

**Benefits of AI in Healthcare:**

1. Improved Diagnosis: AI algorithms can analyze large amounts of medical data to improve diagnosis accuracy, with studies showing a 10-20% increase in diagnostic accuracy.
2. Personalized Medicine: AI can help tailor treatment plans to individual patients based on their unique genetic profiles, medical histories, and lifestyle factors, leading to more effective treatment outcomes.
3. Predictive Analytics: AI-powered predictive models can identify high-risk patients and predict the likelihood of readmission or complications, allowing for targeted interventions that can reduce hospitalization rates by 15-20%.
4. Streamlined Clinical Workflows: AI can automate routine tasks such as data entry, billing, and appointment scheduling, freeing up healthcare professionals to focus on more complex patient care.
5. Increased Efficiency: AI-powered chatbots and virtual assistants can help patients with simple queries, reducing the workload of human staff and improving overall efficiency.

**Challenges and Concerns:**

1. Data Quality and Bias: AI algorithms can perpetuate biases in medical data, leading to unequal treatment for certain populations. A study by the American Medical Association found that AI algorithms can perpetuate biases in medical data, resulting in 10-20% lower treatment outcomes.
2. Regulatory Frameworks: There is a need for clear regulatory frameworks to govern the use of AI in healthcare and ensure patient safety. The FDA has established guidelines for the development and testing of AI-powered medical devices.
3. Cybersecurity Risks: AI systems can be vulnerable to cyber threats, compromising patient data and disrupting care delivery.

**Addressing Challenges:**

To address the challenges associated with AI adoption in healthcare, we recommend:

1. Implementing robust data quality control measures to minimize bias in medical data.
2. Developing clear regulatory frameworks governing the use of AI in healthcare.
3. Investing in cybersecurity measures, such as encryption, firewalls, and regular software updates, to protect AI systems from cyber threats.

**Conclusion:**

Artificial Intelligence (AI) has the potential to revolutionize healthcare by improving diagnosis, treatment, and patient outcomes. However, its adoption is not without challenges and concerns. By addressing these challenges and leveraging opportunities for growth and innovation, we can unlock the full potential of AI in healthcare and improve patient care.

**Recommendations:**

1. Conduct thorough risk assessments and develop mitigation strategies for the identified risks.
2. Establish a multidisciplinary task force to address data quality, regulatory frameworks, and cybersecurity concerns.
3. Invest in training and education programs for healthcare professionals on the benefits and challenges of AI adoption.
4. Foster collaboration between healthcare organizations, regulators, and technology vendors to ensure harmonized standards and best practices.

**Opportunities:**

1. Develop AI-powered diagnostic tools that can detect diseases earlier and more accurately, improving patient outcomes.
2. Create personalized medicine platforms that use AI to develop targeted treatment plans for patients with specific conditions.
3. Implement AI-powered workflow automation solutions that can streamline clinical workflows and improve operational efficiency.
4. Develop AI-powered chatbot platforms that can provide patient support and improve healthcare provider productivity.

**Risks:**

1. Data Quality and Bias: The use of AI algorithms in healthcare can perpetuate biases in medical data, leading to unequal treatment for certain populations.
2. Regulatory Frameworks: The lack of clear regulatory frameworks governing the use of AI in healthcare can lead to uncertainty and confusion among healthcare professionals, patients, and regulators.
3. Cybersecurity Risks: The increasing dependence on AI systems in healthcare can create vulnerabilities to cyber threats, compromising patient data and disrupting care delivery.

**Mitigation Strategies:**

1. Implement robust data quality control measures to minimize bias in medical data.
2. Develop clear regulatory frameworks governing the use of AI in healthcare.
3. Invest in cybersecurity measures, such as encryption, firewalls, and regular software updates, to protect AI systems from cyber threats.

```spl
-- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
-- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
-- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="$(cat myfile.py)"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)" language="Go"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)" language="Python"

WORKFLOW code_review
    INPUT: @code TEXT, @language TEXT
    OUTPUT: @review TEXT
DO
    -- Pass 1: Security audit
    GENERATE security_audit(@code, @language) INTO @security_findings

    -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
    GENERATE performance_review(@code, @language) INTO @perf_findings

    -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
    GENERATE style_review(@code, @language) INTO @style_findings

    -- Pass 4: Bug detection
    GENERATE bug_detection(@code, @language) INTO @bug_findings

    -- Severity scoring for each category
    GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score

    -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
    GENERATE synthesize_review(
        @security_findings, @sec_score,
        @perf_findings, @perf_score,
        @style_findings,
        @bug_findings, @bug_score
    ) INTO @review

    -- Determine overall verdict
    EVALUATE @sec_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
        WHEN > 5 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
        OTHERWISE
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Code too large — review in chunks
        GENERATE summarize_code(@code) INTO @summary
        GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
        COMMIT @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
END
```

```python
import ast

def safe_eval(x):
    """
    Safely evaluates a string as Python code.

    Args:
        x (str): The string to evaluate.

    Returns:
        The result of the evaluated expression.

    Raises:
        ValueError: If the input string cannot be safely evaluated.
    """
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except (ValueError, SyntaxError):
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid syntax: {x}")

def foo(x):
    """
    Evaluates a string as Python code safely using ast.literal_eval.

    Args:
        x (str): The string to evaluate.

    Returns:
        The result of the evaluated expression.

    Raises:
        ValueError: If the input string cannot be safely evaluated.
    """
    return safe_eval(x)
```

     | Changes made:
     | 
     | *  Replaced `eval()` with `ast.literal_eval` for safer evaluation of strings. This function only evaluates literals (like numbers, strings, booleans) and is more secure than `eval`.
     | *  Wrapped the call to `ast.literal_eval` in a `try-except` block to catch any invalid syntax errors.
     | *  Added docstrings to provide documentation for the functions.
     | 
     | **Best Practices**
     | 
     | 1.  Always validate and sanitize user input when working with dynamic expressions or evaluating untrusted input to prevent security vulnerabilities.
     | 2.  Use safer alternatives like `ast.literal_eval()` or libraries like `numexpr` if possible, as they provide more secure evaluation mechanisms without sacrificing performance.

     |   @bug_findings = **Bug Detection and Fixing**
     | 
     | The code provided contains a security vulnerability due to the use of `eval()` function. The `eval()` function parses the expression passed to this method and executes Python expression(s) passed as a string.
     | 
     | In the given code, the input `x` is directly evaluated without any sanitization or validation, which makes it vulnerable to arbitrary code execution attacks. An attacker could provide malicious input that would be executed on the server, leading to potential security breaches.
     | 
     | **Fix: Safe Evaluation Alternative**
     | 
     | To fix this vulnerability, we can use a safer alternative to `eval()`, such as using the `ast` (Abstract Syntax Trees) module or a library like `numexpr`. However, in this example, I will demonstrate how to safely evaluate expressions using the `eval()` function with proper input validation.
     | 
     | **Code Fix:**
     | 
```python
import re

def foo(x):
    # Validate and sanitize user input
    if not isinstance(x, str) or not x.strip():
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")

    # Use a regular expression pattern to restrict allowed characters
    pattern = r'^[a-zA-Z0-9_\.\+\-\*\/\(\)\[\]\{\}\=<>]+$'
    if not re.match(pattern, x):
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")

    try:
        return eval(x)
    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid expression: {e}")
```
     | 
     | In the modified code:
     | 
     | 1.  Input validation is performed using `isinstance()` and `strip()`.
     | 2.  A regular expression pattern (`pattern`) is used to restrict allowed characters in the input string. This ensures that only valid Python expressions are accepted.
     | 3.  The `try`-`except` block catches any exceptions raised during evaluation, providing a more robust error handling mechanism.
     | 
     | **Best Practice:** Always validate and sanitize user input when working with dynamic expressions or evaluating untrusted input to prevent security vulnerabilities.
     |

import ast

def safe_eval(x: str) -> any:
    """
    Safely evaluates a string as Python code.

    Args:
        x (str): The string to evaluate.

    Returns:
        The result of the evaluated expression.

    Raises:
        ValueError: If the input string cannot be safely evaluated.
    """
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except (ValueError, SyntaxError):
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid syntax: {x}")

def foo(x: str) -> any:
    """
    Evaluates a string as Python code safely using ast.literal_eval.

    Args:
        x (str): The string to evaluate.

    Returns:
        The result of the evaluated expression.

    Raises:
        ValueError: If the input string cannot be safely evaluated.
    """
    return safe_eval(x)
```

```spl
-- Recipe 16: Reflection Agent
-- The agent solves a problem, then reflects on its own reasoning to catch errors.
-- If reflection finds issues, it corrects and re-reflects until confident.
-- Demonstrates meta-cognitive loops and self-assessment patterns.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="What are the trade-offs of microservices vs monoliths?"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a URL shortener system"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Explain why bubble sort is O(n^2)"

WORKFLOW reflection_agent
    INPUT: @problem TEXT
    OUTPUT: @answer TEXT
DO
    @iteration := 0
    @max_reflections := 3
    @confidence := 0

    -- Initial attempt at solving the problem
    GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer

    -- Reflection loop: assess and improve
    WHILE @iteration < @max_reflections DO
        -- Reflect: identify errors, gaps, or weak reasoning
        GENERATE reflect(@problem, @answer) INTO @reflection

        -- Score confidence in the answer
        GENERATE confidence_score(@answer, @reflection) INTO @confidence

        EVALUATE @confidence
            WHEN > 0.85 THEN
                COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'confident', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration
            OTHERWISE
                -- Extract specific issues to fix
                GENERATE extract_issues(@reflection) INTO @issues

                -- Correct the answer based on identified issues
                GENERATE correct(@answer, @issues, @problem) INTO @answer

                @iteration := @iteration + 1
        END
    END

    -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort with final confidence
    COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'best_effort', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'max_reflections', confidence = @confidence
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'restarted'
END
```

```output
Here's an improved design for the URL shortener system:

**System Requirements**

*   Store information about all URLs (shortened and original)
*   Generate unique shortened URLs for each input
*   Return the original URL when the shortened URL is requested
*   Implement authentication and authorization mechanisms
*   Handle errors and exceptions properly

**Database Design**

A suitable database design for this system could be:

#### Table: urls

| Field Name | Data Type | Description |
| --- | --- | --- |
| id | int | Unique identifier for the URL |
| original_url | varchar(255) | Original long URL |
| shortened_url | varchar(255) | Shortened version of the URL |

#### Table: analytics

| Field Name | Data Type | Description |
| --- | --- | --- |
| id | int | Unique identifier for an analytics record |
| url_id | int | Foreign key referencing the urls table |
| referrer | varchar(255) | Referring webpage (optional) |
| timestamp | datetime | Timestamp when the shortened URL was accessed |

#### Table: users

| Field Name | Data Type | Description |
| --- | --- | --- |
| id | int | Unique identifier for a user account |
| username | varchar(50) | Username chosen by the user |
| password | varchar(255) | Salted and hashed password for the user |

**Frontend Design**

The frontend of the system will consist of a simple web application that accepts an original long URL, generates a shortened version, and stores it in the database.

#### Code (Python, Flask, PostgreSQL)

```python
from flask import Flask, request, jsonify, g
import sqlite3
import uuid
import redis
from flask_httpauth import HTTPBasicAuth

app = Flask(__name__)

# Create the database connection
conn = sqlite3.connect('urls.db')
c = conn.cursor()
g.conn = conn

# Initialize Redis cache
redis_client = redis.Redis(host='localhost', port=6379, db=0)

# Define the authentication module
auth = HTTPBasicAuth()

@auth.verify_password
def verify_password(username, password):
    c.execute('SELECT * FROM users WHERE username = ?', (username,))
    user = c.fetchone()
    if user and user[2] == hash(password):
        g.user = user[0]
        return True
    return False

# Function to generate a unique shortened URL
def generate_shortened_url(original_url):
    # Use UUID to ensure uniqueness
    return f'http://short.url/{uuid.uuid4().hex[:6]}'

@app.route('/url', methods=['POST'])
@auth.login required
def create_url():
    original_url = request.json['original_url']
    
    # Generate a new shortened URL
    shortened_url = generate_shortened_url(original_url)
    
    # Insert the new URL into the database
    c.execute('INSERT INTO urls (original_url, shortened_url) VALUES (?, ?)', (original_url, shortened_url))
    
    conn.commit()
    
    # Store in Redis cache with expiration time of 1 hour
    redis_client.set(shortened_url, original_url, ex=3600)
    
    return jsonify({'shortened_url': shortened_url})

@app.route('/url/<string:shortened_url>', methods=['GET'])
@auth.login required
def get_original_url(shortened_url):
    # Check if the URL exists in Redis cache
    cached_value = redis_client.get(shortened_url)
    if cached_value:
        return jsonify({'original_url': cached_value.decode('utf-8')})
    
    c.execute('SELECT original_url FROM urls WHERE shortened_url = ?', (shortened_url,))
    result = c.fetchone()
    
    if result:
        # Update the value in Redis cache
        redis_client.set(shortened_url, result[0], ex=3600)
        
        return jsonify({'original_url': result[0]})
    else:
        return jsonify({'error': 'URL not found'}), 404

@app.route('/login', methods=['POST'])
def login():
    username = request.json['username']
    password = request.json['password']
    
    c.execute('SELECT * FROM users WHERE username = ?', (username,))
    user = c.fetchone()
    
    if user and verify_password(username, password):
        return jsonify({'message': 'Login successful'})
    else:
        return jsonify({'error': 'Invalid credentials'}), 401

if __name__ == "__main__":
    app.run(debug=True)
```
     | 
     | **Security Considerations**
     | 
     | To enhance security:
     | 
     | 1. Implement authentication and authorization mechanisms (e.g., HTTP Basic Auth, OAuth).
     | 2. Use parameterized queries or an ORM library to protect against SQL injection attacks.
     | 3. Handle errors and exceptions properly (e.g., return meaningful error messages, log errors).
     | 4. Implement rate limiting using a library like Flask-Limiter.
     | 
     | **API
     | ```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @problem = Design a URL shortener system
     |   @iteration = 3
     |   @max_reflections = 3
     |   @confidence = The provided Python code using Flask and SQLite for a URL shortener system has some strengths:
     | 
     | 1.  **Easy to Understand**: The code is well-structured and easy to understand, with clear comments explaining each section.
     | 2.  **Secure by Default**: Implementing rate limiting and caching can help protect the system against abuse and improve performance.

     | 
     | 1.  **Input Validation**: Validate user input to ensure it's a valid HTTP URL.
     | 2.  **SQL Injection Protection**: Use parameterized queries or an ORM library (e.g., Flask-SQLAlchemy) to protect against SQL injection attacks.
     | 3.  **Rate Limiting**: Implement rate limiting using a library like Flask-Limiter to prevent abuse and improve performance.
     | 4.  **Cache**: Consider implementing caching using Redis or Memcached to store frequently accessed URLs and reduce the load on your database.
     | 5.  **Authentication and Authorization**: Implement authentication and authorization mechanisms, such as Flask-HTTPAuth or OAuth, to protect user accounts.

     | 1.  `POST /url`: Create a new URL and generate a shortened version.
     | 2.  `GET /url/{shortened_url}`: Return the original URL for a given shortened URL.
     |

```bash
spl run ./17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem=Should we rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor?
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 17: Tree of Thought
-- Explores multiple reasoning paths in parallel, evaluates each, and selects the best.
-- Demonstrates branching exploration, comparative evaluation, and path scoring.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="How should a startup prioritize between growth and profitability?"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a caching strategy for a high-traffic API"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Should we rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor?"

WORKFLOW tree_of_thought
    INPUT: @problem TEXT
    OUTPUT: @best_solution TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Generate multiple initial approaches
    GENERATE approach_1(@problem) INTO @path_a
    GENERATE approach_2(@problem) INTO @path_b
    GENERATE approach_3(@problem) INTO @path_c

    -- Step 2: Develop each approach one level deeper
    GENERATE develop(@path_a, @problem) INTO @path_a_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_b, @problem) INTO @path_b_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_c, @problem) INTO @path_c_developed

    -- Step 3: Score each path on multiple criteria
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_a_developed, @problem) INTO @score_a
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_b_developed, @problem) INTO @score_b
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_c_developed, @problem) INTO @score_c

    -- Step 4: Select the best path
    GENERATE select_best(
        @path_a_developed, @score_a,
        @path_b_developed, @score_b,
        @path_c_developed, @score_c
    ) INTO @best_path

    -- Step 5: Refine the winning path into a final solution
    GENERATE refine_solution(@best_path, @problem) INTO @best_solution

    -- Step 6: Verify the solution holds up
    GENERATE verify(@best_solution, @problem) INTO @verification

    EVALUATE @verification
        WHEN 'sound' THEN
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'complete', paths_explored = 3
        OTHERWISE
            -- If verification fails, combine insights from all paths
            GENERATE synthesize_all(
                @path_a_developed, @path_b_developed, @path_c_developed, @problem
            ) INTO @best_solution
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'synthesized', paths_explored = 3
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- Return whatever path was scored highest so far
        COMMIT @best_path WITH status = 'budget_limit'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END
```

1. The problem description mentions that there are two main options for addressing the legacy system: rewriting it from scratch (Option A) and incrementally refactoring (Option B). Incremental refactoring seems like a more feasible option given the information provided.

Incremental refactoring appears to be a more cost-effective and efficient approach that can deliver value faster without rewriting the entire system from scratch.
3. There's no explicit statement about the complexity or monolithic nature of the legacy system. However, incremental refactoring can still be applied even if the system is complex or has multiple components.

     |   @path_b = **Approach: Incremental Refactoring**
     | 
     | When deciding whether to rewrite a legacy system or incrementally refactor it, there are several factors to consider. Here's a step-by-step approach:
     | 
     | ### Assess the Legacy System
     | 
     | 1. **Evaluate its complexity**: Is the system complex and tightly coupled, making it difficult to understand and modify?
     | 2. **Assess its maintainability**: Can the system be kept in the short term, or is it nearing the end of its lifespan?
     | 3. **Identify critical components**: Are there specific parts of the system that are essential and should be preserved?
     | 
     | ### Consider Incremental Refactoring
     | 
     | 1. **Focus on small, isolated changes**: Instead of trying to overhaul the entire system at once, focus on making targeted improvements.
     | 2. **Update dependencies**: Replace outdated or incompatible dependencies with newer versions.
     | 3. **Simplify architecture**: Gradually refactor the system's architecture to make it more modular and maintainable.
     | 
     | ### Benefits of Incremental Refactoring
     | 
     | 1. **Reduced risk**: By making incremental changes, you can mitigate the risks associated with rewriting a complex system.
     | 2. **Improved maintainability**: As you refactored incrementally, the system becomes easier to understand and modify.
     | 3. **Faster time-to-value**: With smaller, focused improvements, you can deliver value sooner.
     | 
     | ### When to Rewrite
     | 
     | 1. **System is too large or complex for incremental refactoring**: If the system is too massive or interconnected, rewriting it might be a better option.
     | 2. **Legacy system is no longer supported**: If the original development team is no longer available, and the system's technology is obsolete, rewriting might be necessary.
     | 
     | ### Example Use Case
     | 
     | Suppose we have an e-commerce platform built on a legacy technology stack that's nearing the end of its lifespan. Instead of rewriting the entire system from scratch, we decide to incrementally refactor it. We start by replacing outdated dependencies with newer versions and simplifying the architecture to make it more modular.
     | 
     | ### Code Example (Python)
     | 
```python
# Before refactoring (legacy code)
import py2exe

class EcommercePlatform:
    def __init__(self):
        self.products = []
        self.process_payments()

    def process_payments(self):
        # Complex logic for processing payments
        pass
```

```python
# After refactoring (refactored code)
import requests

class ProductManager:
    def __init__(self, api_url):
        self.api_url = api_url
        self.products = []

    def fetch_products(self):
        response = requests.get(f"{self.api_url}/products")
        # Simplify the logic for fetching products
        return response.json()

# Usage
if __name__ == "__main__":
    api_url = "https://example.com/api"
    manager = ProductManager(api_url)
    products = manager.fetch_products()
    print(products)
```

     | In this example, we've refactored a simple e-commerce platform to make it more modular and maintainable. We've replaced the complex `process_payments` method with a simpler API call using `requests`. This incremental refactoring approach allows us to deliver value sooner without rewriting the entire system from scratch.
     | 
     | ### Conclusion
     | 
     | Incremental refactoring is an effective approach for legacy systems that requires careful consideration of its benefits and drawbacks. By focusing on small, isolated changes and simplifying architecture, you can improve maintainability while reducing risk and delivering value faster. However, if the system is too large or complex for incremental refactoring, rewriting it might be a better option.
     |   @path_c = Based on my knowledge cutoff in 2023, here are some key considerations to approach this task:
     | 
     | **Approach 3: Incremental Refactoring**
     | 
     | Incremental refactoring is a viable option for modernizing legacy systems. This approach involves breaking down the system into smaller components, identifying areas that can be improved or replaced with new technologies, and then incrementally updating those components.
     | 
     | **Pros of incremental refactoring:**
     | 
     | 1. Reduced risk: By making small changes at a time, you can test and validate each component before moving on to the next.
     | 2. Improved maintainability: Incremental refactoring helps to create a more modular and maintainable system.
     | 3. Cost-effective: Refactoring components individually is often less expensive than rewriting the entire system.
     | 
     | **Cons of incremental refactoring:**
     | 
     | 1. Time-consuming: Breaking down the system into smaller components can be time-consuming and labor-intensive.
     | 2. Complexity: As you refactor individual components, it's essential to ensure that the changes don't introduce new complexity or dependencies.
     | 
     | **When to choose incremental refactoring:**
     | 
     | 1. Systems with relatively simple architecture
     | 2. Small-to-medium-sized teams
     | 3. When the business needs are stable and not changing rapidly
     | 
     | **When to consider rewriting the legacy system:**
     | 
     | 1. Complex or monolithic systems that can't be broken down into smaller components.
     | 2. Rapidly changing business requirements
     | 3. Significant infrastructure changes, such as migrating from on-premises to cloud-based services.

```spl
-- Recipe 18: Guardrails Pipeline
-- Input validation → safe generation → output validation → response.
-- Demonstrates safety-first patterns: content filtering, PII detection, and output sanitization.
-- Four gates before a response is committed.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=safe_encryption
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=pii_ssn
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=harmful_malware
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       user_input="My SSN is 123-45-6789, help me file taxes"
--
-- Discover available test inputs:
--   spl run ... input_id=list

WORKFLOW guardrails_pipeline
    INPUT:  @input_id   TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @user_input TEXT DEFAULT ''
    OUTPUT: @safe_response TEXT
    SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
    -- Step 1: Load test input from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- If input_id is blank, @user_input is used directly in subsequent steps.
    CALL load_test_input(@input_id) INTO @test_context

    -- Gate 1a: Keyword pre-screen (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Fast check for obvious harmful / off-topic patterns before spending tokens.
    CALL classify_input_keywords(@user_input) INTO @keyword_class

    EVALUATE @keyword_class
        WHEN 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate   = 'keyword_prescreen'
        WHEN 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate   = 'keyword_prescreen'
        OTHERWISE
            -- Continue to LLM classification
    END

    -- Gate 1b: LLM input content classification (nuanced cases keywords miss)
    GENERATE classify_input(@user_input, @test_context) INTO @input_class

    EVALUATE @input_class
        WHEN 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        WHEN 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        OTHERWISE
            -- Input is acceptable, continue
    END

    -- Gate 2: PII detection (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL detect_pii(@user_input) INTO @pii_report

    EVALUATE @pii_report
        WHEN 'pii_found' THEN
            -- Gate 2b: Redact PII (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL redact_pii(@user_input) INTO @clean_input
        OTHERWISE
            SET @clean_input = @user_input
    END

    -- Gate 3: Generate response with safety-tuned prompt
    GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @raw_response

    -- Gate 4: Output validation — check for PII leakage or harmful content
    GENERATE validate_output(@raw_response, @clean_input) INTO @output_check

    EVALUATE @output_check
        WHEN 'safe' THEN
            SET @safe_response = @raw_response
        WHEN 'contains_pii' THEN
            -- Deterministic PII redaction on the output too
            CALL redact_pii(@raw_response) INTO @safe_response
        WHEN 'hallucination' THEN
            GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @safe_response
            RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
        OTHERWISE
            SET @safe_response = 'I was unable to generate a safe response. Please rephrase your question.'
    END

    COMMIT @safe_response WITH
        status       = 'complete',
        input_class  = @input_class,
        pii_detected = @pii_report

EXCEPTION
    WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
        COMMIT 'This request cannot be processed.' WITH status = 'refused'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        COMMIT 'Unable to provide a verified response.' WITH status = 'hallucination_blocked'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @safe_response WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

```python
# Define a dictionary that maps keywords to their respective categories
keyword_categories = {
    "explain": ["encryption", "security"],
    "how": ["encryption", "technology"],
    "to": ["encryption", "information"]
}

def classify_input_keywords(input_string):
    """
    Classify the input string into its corresponding keyword category.

    Args:
        input_string (str): The user's input string.

    Returns:
        list: A list of categories for each keyword found in the input string.
    """
    # Initialize an empty dictionary to store the results
    result = {}

    # Split the input string into individual words
    words = input_string.split()

    # Iterate over each word in the input string
    for word in words:
        # Check if the word is a keyword (i.e., it exists in our keyword_categories dictionary)
        if word.lower() in [keyword.lower() for keyword in keyword_categories.keys()]:
            # Find the category associated with the keyword
            for category, keywords in keyword_categories.items():
                if word.lower() in [k.lower() for k in keywords]:
                    result[word] = category

    return result


# Example usage:
input_string = "explain how encryption works to secure data"

result = classify_input_keywords(input_string)

for keyword, category in result.items():
    print(f"{keyword}: {category}")
```

     | ```
     | explain: security
     | how: encryption
     | works: 
     | to: information
     | ```

     | This is the result of the `classify_input_keywords` procedure. The input string was split into individual words and each word was checked against our dictionary to determine its corresponding category.
     | 
     | Note that this is a simple example, and in a real-world scenario, you would use a more sophisticated approach to classify keywords, such as using natural language processing (NLP) techniques or machine learning algorithms.

```spl
-- Recipe 19: Memory-Augmented Conversation
-- A conversational agent that remembers facts from prior turns using persistent memory.
-- Demonstrates memory.get/memory.set, context accumulation, and stateful interactions.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="My name is Alice and I'm a data scientist"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="What's my name?"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="I prefer Python over R"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="What do you know about me?"
--
-- Memory persists across runs via `spl memory` store.
-- Reset with: spl memory delete chat_user_profile

WORKFLOW memory_conversation
    INPUT: @user_input TEXT
    OUTPUT: @response TEXT
DO
    -- Load existing user profile from memory
    SELECT memory.get('chat_user_profile') AS profile INTO @profile
    SELECT memory.get('chat_history') AS chat_history INTO @chat_history

    -- Extract any new facts the user shared about themselves
    GENERATE extract_facts(@user_input) INTO @new_facts

    -- Update profile with new facts (if any)
    EVALUATE @new_facts
        WHEN 'no_new_facts' THEN
            -- Nothing to update
        OTHERWISE
            GENERATE merge_profile(@profile, @new_facts) INTO @profile
            -- STORE @profile IN memory.chat_user_profile  -- workflow-level memory write not yet supported
    END

    -- Generate response with full context: profile + history + current input
    GENERATE contextual_reply(@user_input, @profile, @chat_history) INTO @response

    -- Update chat history (keep last 10 turns)
    @chat_history := @chat_history + '\nUser: ' + @user_input + '\nAssistant: ' + @response
    GENERATE trim_history(@chat_history, 10) INTO @chat_history
    -- STORE @chat_history IN memory.chat_history  -- workflow-level memory write not yet supported

    COMMIT @response WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT 'I remember you! But I ran out of budget for this response.' WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END

```spl
-- Recipe 20: Ensemble Voting
-- Generates multiple independent answers, then uses majority voting to pick the best.
-- Demonstrates ensemble methods, comparative scoring, and consensus-driven output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 question="What causes inflation?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="Is Rust faster than C++?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the best database for time-series data?"

WORKFLOW ensemble_voting
    INPUT: @question TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_answer TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Generate 5 independent candidate answers with varying temperatures
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_1
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_2
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_3
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_4
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_5

    -- Step 2: Score each candidate on accuracy, completeness, and clarity
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_1, @question) INTO @score_1
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_2, @question) INTO @score_2
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_3, @question) INTO @score_3
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_4, @question) INTO @score_4
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_5, @question) INTO @score_5

    -- Step 3: Find consensus — identify common themes across candidates
    GENERATE find_consensus(
        @candidate_1, @candidate_2, @candidate_3,
        @candidate_4, @candidate_5
    ) INTO @consensus

    -- Step 4: Select the best candidate based on scores and consensus alignment
    GENERATE select_winner(
        @candidate_1, @score_1,
        @candidate_2, @score_2,
        @candidate_3, @score_3,
        @candidate_4, @score_4,
        @candidate_5, @score_5,
        @consensus
    ) INTO @best_candidate

    -- Step 5: Polish the winning answer, incorporating consensus insights
    GENERATE polish(@best_candidate, @consensus, @question) INTO @final_answer

    COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'complete', candidates = 5

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- If budget runs out, use whatever candidates we have
        GENERATE select_winner(
            @candidate_1, @score_1,
            @candidate_2, @score_2,
            @candidate_3, @score_3
        ) INTO @final_answer
        COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'partial', candidates = 3
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END
```

```output
Based on the provided information, it appears that Input 1 is a function that calculates a score for selecting a winner in an inflation context. The input includes scores for different causes of inflation.

To polish this code and make it more efficient and readable, here's a refactored version:

```python
def calculate_winner_score(input1, input2, input3):
    """
    Calculate the total score for selecting a winner in an inflation context.
    
    Args:
        input1 (list): List of strings representing causes of inflation.
        input2 (list): List of strings representing common causes across all inputs.
        input3 (list): List of strings representing additional causes from Input 3.
    
    Returns:
        float: The total score for selecting a winner.
    """
    
    # Define scores for each cause of inflation
    inflation_scores = {
        "Demand and Supply Imbalance": 8,
        "Monetary Policy": 9,
        "Cost-push Inflation": 7,
        "Supply Chain Disruptions": 6,
        "Economic Growth": 8,
        "Fiscal Policy": 7
    }
    
    # Define common causes across all inputs
    common_causes = set(input1) & set(input2) & set(input3)
    
    # Calculate the total score based on the type of inflation
    if len(common_causes) > 0:
        if "Monetary Policy" in common_causes and (
            set("Demand and Supply Imbalance" in input1 for x in input1) & 
            set("demand-pull inflation" in input2 for x in input2) & 
            set("monetary policy" in input3 for x in input3) 
        ):
            total_score = sum(inflation_scores.values()) / len(inflation_scores)
        elif any(x == "Demand and Supply Imbalance" for x in common_causes) and (
            set("demand-pull inflation" in input2 for x in input2) & 
            set("cost-push inflation" in input2 for x in input2) & 
            set("monetary policy" in input3 for x in input3)
        ):
            total_score = (8 + 9 + 7) / 3
        elif any(x == "Demand-pull inflation" or x == "cost-push inflation" for x in common_causes) and (
            set("monetary policy" in input2 for x in input2) & 
            set("economic growth" in input3 for x in input3)
        ):
            total_score = (9 + 8) / 2
        elif any(x == "cost-push inflation" or x == "economic growth" for x in common_causes) and (
            set("monetary policy" in input2 for x in input2) & 
            set("demand-pull inflation" in input3 for x in input3)
        ):
            total_score = (8 + 7) / 2
    else:
        total_score = None
    
    return total_score

# Define scores for each cause from Input5, Input8
inflation_scores_input5_8 = {
    "Commodity Price Increases": 5,
    "External Shocks": 4
}

def select_winner():
    # Calculate the total score
    if len(set(Input1)) & len(set(Input2)) & len(set(common_causes)) & len(set(common_causes_input4)):
        common_causes = set(Input1) & set(Input2) & set(common_causes)
        
        if all([input_i == "monetary policy" or input_i == "demand and supply imbalance" for input_i in Input1]) and \
           all([input_i == "demand-pull inflation" or input_i == "cost-push inflation" or input_i == "monetary policy" for input_i in Input2]) and \
           all([input_i == "monetary policy" or input_i == "economic growth" or input_i == "supply chain disruptions" 
                for input_i in Input3]) and \
           all([input_i == "demand and supply imbalance" or input_i == "monetary policy" 
                for input_i in common_causes_input4]):
            total_score = calculate_winner_score(Input1, Input2, Input3)
        elif all([input_i == "monetary policy" or input_i == "demand and supply imbalance" 
                 for input_i in Input1]) and \
             all([input_i == "demand-pull inflation" or input_i == "cost-push inflation" or input_i == "monetary policy"
                  for input_i in Input2]) and \
             all([input_i == "monetary policy" or input_i == "economic growth" or input_i == "supply chain disruptions" 
                 
```

[21] Multi-Model Pipeline

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 21: Multi-Model Pipeline
-- Showcases GENERATE ... USING MODEL per-step model selection
-- and semantic WHILE loop with variable context.
--
-- Each step can target a different model — the right model for each task.
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic="climate change"

CREATE FUNCTION research(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a research specialist.
Provide key facts, statistics, and recent developments about: {topic}
Focus on factual accuracy. Be thorough but concise.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION analyze(facts TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a data analyst.
Analyze the following research and identify the 3 most significant insights:

{facts}

For each insight, explain why it matters and rate its significance (1-10).
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION write_summary(analysis TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a professional writer.
Write a clear, engaging 2-paragraph summary based on this analysis:

{analysis}

The first paragraph should present the key findings.
The second paragraph should discuss implications and outlook.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION quality_check(text TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a quality reviewer.
Rate the following text on a scale of 0.0 to 1.0 for:
- Clarity
- Accuracy
- Completeness

Return ONLY a single number (the average score).

Text: {text}
$$;

WORKFLOW multi_model_pipeline
  INPUT: @topic TEXT
  OUTPUT: @final TEXT
DO
  -- Step 1: Research with a fast model (good for factual retrieval)
  GENERATE research(@topic) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @facts;

  -- Step 2: Analysis with a reasoning model
  GENERATE analyze(@facts) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @analysis;

  -- Step 3: Writing with a creative model
  GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @draft;

  -- Step 4: Quality loop — refine until score > 0.7 (max 3 iterations)
  @iteration := 0;
  @quality := 0;

  WHILE @iteration < 3 DO
    GENERATE quality_check(@draft) INTO @quality;

    EVALUATE @quality
      WHEN > 0.7 THEN
        COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'high_quality', score = @quality
      OTHERWISE
        GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) INTO @draft
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
    END
  END

  -- Fallback: commit whatever we have
  COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'max_iterations', score = @quality

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN ModelOverloaded THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'model_overloaded'
END;
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 4
     | Tokens: 2033 in / 1825 out
     | Latency: 32313ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Here’s a two-paragraph summary based on the provided analysis, designed to be clear, engaging, and impactful:

Recent research has unequivocally established a critical reality: 2023 marked the hottest year on record, boasting a staggering 1.48°C increase in global temperatures compared to pre-industrial levels. Alongside this alarming statistic, the analysis reveals two crucial underlying mechanisms driving this rapid warming. First, the Arctic is experiencing amplified warming, roughly twice the global average, creating a powerful feedback loop where melting ice absorbs more solar radiation. Second, permafrost across the globe is thawing at an accelerated pace, releasing substantial quantities of greenhouse gases – primarily methane and carbon dioxide – further exacerbating the warming trend. These findings collectively paint a picture of a climate system under immense stress, with consequences already being felt with unprecedented severity.

The implications of these insights are profound and demand immediate attention. The combination of record-breaking heat, Arctic amplification, and permafrost thaw represents a potentially runaway feedback loop, significantly increasing the risk of exceeding critical warming thresholds. While the data highlights the urgency of the situation, it also underscores the complex and interconnected nature of the climate crisis. Addressing these challenges requires a concerted global effort focused on drastic reductions in greenhouse gas emissions, coupled with innovative strategies to mitigate the effects of ongoing warming and stabilize the most vulnerable components of the Earth’s system.
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @topic = climate change
     |   @facts = Okay, here’s a thorough but concise overview of climate change, incorporating key facts, statistics, and recent developments. This information is based on the latest reports from the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC), NASA, NOAA, and other reputable scientific organizations.
     | 
     | **I. The Science of Climate Change – The Basics**
     | 
     | * **The Greenhouse Effect:**  The Earth's atmosphere naturally traps some of the sun's energy, keeping the planet warm enough to support life. This is the greenhouse effect.  Greenhouse gases (GHGs) like carbon dioxide (CO2), methane (CH4), and nitrous oxide (N2O) are responsible.
     | * **Human Activities as the Primary Driver:** Since the Industrial Revolution (around 1750), human activities, primarily the burning of fossil fuels (coal, oil, and natural gas) for energy, have dramatically increased the concentration of GHGs in the atmosphere.
     | * **Observed Warming:** The planet has warmed by approximately 1.1°C (2°F) since the pre-industrial era (1850-1900). The vast majority of this warming is unequivocally caused by human influence. (Source: IPCC Sixth Assessment Report - AR6)
     | * **Key Indicators of Change:**
     |     * **Global Surface Temperature Rise:** The average global surface temperature has risen by 1.09°C (1.96°F) between 1850-2019.
     |     * **Sea Level Rise:** Global mean sea level has risen by about 21-26 centimeters (8-10 inches) since 1880, largely due to thermal expansion of water and melting of glaciers and ice sheets.
     |     * **Melting Ice:** Arctic sea ice extent has declined by about 13% per decade since 1979.  Greenland and Antarctic ice sheets are losing mass at an accelerating rate.
     |     * **Extreme Weather:**  There is increasing evidence that climate change is intensifying extreme weather events, including heatwaves, heavy rainfall, droughts, and wildfires.
     | 
     | 
     | **II. Recent Statistics & Trends (2023-2024)**
     | 
     | * **2023 Was the Hottest Year on Record:**  Global average temperatures in 2023 were 1.48°C (2.66°F) above pre-industrial levels. This was the warmest year on record, according to NASA and NOAA.
     | * **Record Ocean Heat:** 2023 saw record-breaking ocean heat content, exacerbating marine ecosystems.
     | * **Continued GHG Emission Increases (2023):** Global GHG emissions reached a new record high in 2023, though the rate of increase has slowed slightly. (IEA data)
     | * **Arctic Amplification:** The Arctic is warming at roughly twice the global average rate.
     | * **Permafrost Thaw:**  Permafrost (permanently frozen ground) is thawing at an accelerated pace, releasing significant amounts of methane and CO2 – a positive feedback loop.
     | 
     | 
     | **III.  Regional Impacts**
     | 
     | * **Coastal Regions:** Sea level rise threatens coastal communities with increased flooding, erosion, and saltwater intrusion.
     | * **Africa:**  Increased drought, desertification, and water scarcity are major concerns.
     | * **Europe:**  Heatwaves are becoming more frequent and intense.
     | * **Asia:**  Melting Himalayan glaciers pose a significant threat to water resources for billions of people.
     | * **North America:**  Increased wildfire risk, longer wildfire seasons, and shifts in agricultural patterns.
     | 
     | **IV.  Key Organizations & Reports**
     | 
     | * **IPCC (Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change):** The leading international body for assessing climate change.  Produces comprehensive assessment reports every 7-10 years. (www.ipcc.ch)
     | * **NASA (National Aeronautics and Space Administration):** Provides extensive data and research on climate change. (climate.nasa.gov)
     | * **NOAA (National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration):**  Collects and analyzes climate data. (www.noaa.gov)
     | * **World Meteorological Organization (WMO):** Coordinates global weather and climate observations. (public.wmo.int)
     | 
     | 
     | **V.  Important Note: Scientific Consensus**
     | 
     | It’s crucial to emphasize that there is overwhelming scientific consensus (over 97%) that climate change is happening and is primarily caused by human activities.  This consensus is based on decades of research and data.
     | 
     | ---
     | 
     | **Disclaimer:** *This information is a summary based on current scientific understanding. Climate science is constantly evolving, and new data and research are continually emerging.  For the most up-to-date information, please refer to the sources mentioned above.*
     | 
     | Would you like me
     |   @analysis = Okay, here’s my analysis of the provided research, identifying the 3 most significant insights and providing an explanation and significance rating for each:
     | 
     | **1. Insight: 2023 Was the Hottest Year on Record (1.48°C above pre-industrial levels).**
     | 
     | * **Explanation:** This statistic is undeniably impactful. It’s not just a number; it represents a dramatic acceleration in the rate of warming. It confirms that the trend of increasing global temperatures is intensifying and that the impacts of climate change are already being felt with unprecedented severity. The fact that 2023 broke previous records highlights the urgency of the situation.
     | * **Significance Rating:** 10/10 – This is the most significant insight due to its direct and alarming demonstration of the current state of the climate crisis.
     | 
     | 
     | **2. Insight: Arctic Amplification – The Arctic is warming at roughly twice the global average rate.**
     | 
     | * **Explanation:** Arctic amplification is a critical feedback loop. Because the Arctic has less ice and snow cover, it absorbs more solar radiation than the rest of the planet. This increased warming then leads to further melting of ice, which, as we’ve seen, accelerates warming even more.  It's a powerful illustration of how seemingly localized changes have global consequences.
     | * **Significance Rating:** 9/10 – This insight is incredibly important because it reveals a self-reinforcing process that dramatically amplifies the effects of global warming.  It’s a key driver of many of the other observed changes.
     | 
     | 
     | 
     | **3. Insight: Permafrost Thaw – Permafrost is thawing at an accelerated pace, releasing significant amounts of methane and CO2 – a positive feedback loop.**
     | 
     | * **Explanation:** The thawing of permafrost represents a potentially catastrophic positive feedback loop. Permafrost contains vast quantities of previously frozen organic matter. As it thaws, this organic matter decomposes, releasing massive amounts of greenhouse gases (primarily methane and CO2) into the atmosphere. This further contributes to global warming, leading to even more permafrost thaw – a vicious cycle.
     | * **Significance Rating:** 8/10 – This insight is highly significant because it introduces a potentially uncontrollable element to the climate system. The release of large quantities of greenhouse gasses from thawing permafrost could dramatically accelerate warming beyond current projections. 
     | 
     | 
     |

     |

     | *   Analyze the data in a different way (e.g., by region or sector)?
     | *   Generate a summary report based on these findings?
     |   @iteration = 0

-- Recipe 23: Structured Output
-- Extract typed, structured data from free-form text using CREATE FUNCTION with JSON schema.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained generation — the LLM must return valid JSON matching the spec.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="John Smith, 42, joined Acme Corp in March 2021 earning $95,000/year"
--
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="Invoice #INV-2045 from TechSupplies Ltd dated 15 Jan 2024 for $3,420.50 due in 30 days"

-- Define the extraction schema
CREATE FUNCTION extract_entity_schema()
RETURNS JSON AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "people": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":       { "type": "string" },
          "age":        { "type": "integer" },
          "role":       { "type": "string" },
          "salary":     { "type": "number" },
          "start_date": { "type": "string", "format": "date" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "organizations": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":     { "type": "string" },
          "type":     { "type": "string" },
          "industry": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "amounts": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value":    { "type": "number" },
          "currency": { "type": "string", "default": "USD" },
          "label":    { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    },
    "dates": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value": { "type": "string" },
          "label": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    }
  }
}
$$;

PROMPT extract_entities
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise data extraction engine. Extract all entities from the text and return valid JSON matching the provided schema. Return only the JSON object, no explanation.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extract_entity_schema() AS schema
GENERATE structured_extraction(text, schema)

```spl
-- Recipe 24: Few-Shot Prompting
-- Embed gold-standard examples directly in the SELECT context to guide output format and style.
-- Demonstrates in-context learning — no fine-tuning required.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="The quarterly results exceeded all analyst forecasts by a significant margin"
--
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="System outage detected in EU-WEST-2 affecting 15% of users" \
--       domain="ops"

-- Gold-standard examples for tone/format calibration
CREATE FUNCTION few_shot_examples(domain TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE domain
  WHEN 'finance' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Revenue grew 12% YoY despite headwinds in APAC."
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.7, "topics": ["revenue", "growth", "APAC"], "summary": "Revenue up 12% YoY."}

Input: "Write-downs of $2.3B drove a net loss for Q3."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.9, "topics": ["write-downs", "net loss", "Q3"], "summary": "Q3 net loss from $2.3B write-downs."}'

  WHEN 'ops' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Deployment succeeded. All health checks passing. P99 latency 45ms."
Output: {"status": "healthy", "severity": "none", "action_required": false, "summary": "Deploy OK, latency nominal."}

Input: "Pod crash-loop in prod-east. OOMKilled. Restarted 7 times in 10 min."
Output: {"status": "incident", "severity": "high", "action_required": true, "summary": "OOM crash-loop in prod-east, immediate attention needed."}'

  ELSE
    '### Examples

Input: "The new feature launched today and users are loving it!"
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.8, "summary": "Successful feature launch with positive user reception."}

Input: "We encountered several critical bugs that delayed the release by two weeks."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.7, "summary": "Critical bugs caused two-week release delay."}'
END
$$;

PROMPT few_shot_classifier
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise text classifier. Follow the examples exactly — same JSON structure, same field names. Return only the JSON object.'),
    few_shot_examples(context.domain) AS examples,
    context.text AS text,
    context.domain AS domain
GENERATE classify(text, examples)
```

     | Tokens: 77 in / 35 out
     | Latency: 618ms
     | Cost: $0.000000

  "text": "The quarterly results exceeded all analyst forecasts by a significant margin",
  "domain": "finance"
}

```spl
-- Recipe 25: Nested Procedures
-- Demonstrates PROCEDURE calling PROCEDURE — deep composability without external orchestration.
-- Pattern: a high-level workflow delegates subtasks to reusable PROCEDURE units.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       topic="quantum computing" audience="high school students"
--
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama \
--       topic="CRISPR gene editing" audience="policy makers" depth="deep"

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a single explanation layer
PROCEDURE explain_layer(content TEXT, audience TEXT, style TEXT DEFAULT 'clear and engaging')
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE explain(content, audience, style) INTO @explanation
    COMMIT @explanation
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a concrete example for a concept
PROCEDURE make_example(concept TEXT, context TEXT, audience TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE concrete_example(concept, context, audience) INTO @example
    COMMIT @example
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: check comprehension level and simplify if needed
PROCEDURE calibrate_complexity(text TEXT, audience TEXT, target_grade INT DEFAULT 8)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE assess_reading_level(text) INTO @reading_level
    EVALUATE @reading_level
        WHEN > target_grade THEN
            GENERATE simplify(text, audience) INTO @calibrated
            COMMIT @calibrated
        OTHERWISE
            COMMIT text
    END
END

-- Outer WORKFLOW: orchestrates the inner procedures
WORKFLOW layered_explainer
    INPUT: @topic TEXT, @audience TEXT, @depth TEXT DEFAULT 'standard'
    OUTPUT: @article TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research the topic
    GENERATE research_overview(@topic) INTO @overview

    -- Step 2: Delegate explanation to inner procedure
    CALL explain_layer(
        content  = @overview,
        audience = @audience,
        style    = 'clear, engaging, avoid jargon'
    ) INTO @base_explanation

    -- Step 3: Generate a concrete example via another inner procedure
    CALL make_example(
        concept  = @topic,
        context  = @base_explanation,
        audience = @audience
    ) INTO @example

    -- Step 4: Calibrate complexity (nested call inside a conditional)
    CALL calibrate_complexity(
        text         = @base_explanation,
        audience     = @audience,
        target_grade = 8
    ) INTO @calibrated_explanation

    -- Step 5: Assemble the final article
    GENERATE assemble_article(
        @topic,
        @calibrated_explanation,
        @example,
        @depth
    ) INTO @article

    COMMIT @article WITH status = 'complete', audience = @audience
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 5
     | Tokens: 2411 in / 2428 out
     | Latency: 32428ms

**Assembled Article**

What is Quantum Computing?

Imagine you have a superpower that allows you to process information way faster and more accurately than any regular computer. That's basically what quantum computing is - a new way of thinking about computers that uses the strange and amazing rules of quantum mechanics.

How Does it Work?

Regular computers use things called "bits" to store information, which can only be either 0 or 1. But with quantum computers, we have something called "qubits" (quantum bits) that can be both 0 AND 1 at the same time! This means that a qubit can process multiple possibilities all at once, making it much faster than regular computers.

What's Next?

Researchers are working on three main areas:

1. Solving Big Problems: They're trying to develop special algorithms (like recipes for the computer) that can solve very hard problems, like cracking secret codes or searching through huge amounts of data.
2. Making it Reliable: Because quantum computers are still really new and fragile, scientists need to figure out ways to make them work correctly even when they get a little messed up.
3. Understanding the World: Quantum computers can help us simulate things that are too complicated or impossible for regular computers to handle, like super-powerful atoms or weird behavior in materials.

What's Making Waves?

Some cool breakthroughs include:

* Google figuring out how to make quantum computers work really fast and accurate
* IBM creating a special platform where developers can play around with quantum computers online

Challenges Ahead

But it's not all sunshine and rainbows - scientists are still working on making these things smaller, faster, and more reliable. They also need to find ways to use them for real-world problems.

What Can We Expect?

Quantum computing has the potential to revolutionize areas like chemistry, materials science, and even machine learning!

**Educational Context**

The concept of quantum computing can be introduced to high school students in an engaging and interactive way. By exploring its basics, applications, and challenges, students can develop a deeper understanding of this cutting-edge technology.

In a lesson titled "Introduction to Quantum Computing: Exploring its Potential," students can:

1. Learn about the fundamental principles of quantum computing
2. Understand how qubits work and their potential for speed and accuracy
3. Explore real-world applications of quantum computing in various fields
4. Discuss the challenges scientists face when developing reliable and scalable quantum computers

By following this lesson plan, high school students will gain a solid foundation in quantum computing and be inspired to learn more about its exciting possibilities.

**Key Terms:**

* Qubits (quantum bits)
* Superposition
* Entanglement
* Quantum gates
* Algorithms
* Solving big problems
* Reliability
* Scalability

**Further Exploration:**

* Watch videos or animations that illustrate how quantum computers work
* Explore online resources, such as articles and websites, for further learning about quantum computing
* Participate in online forums or discussions to learn from experts and enthusiasts

[26] Prompt A/B Test
     cmd : spl run ./26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama task=Explain neural networks prompt_a=Explain like I'm 5 prompt_b=Give a technical explanation with analogies
     log : /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/Cookbook-of-SPL-Recipes/references/SPL20/cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test_20260323_010238.log
     | 
```bash
spl run ./26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 task=Explain neural networks prompt_a=Explain like I'm 5 prompt_b=Give a technical explanation with analogies
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 26: Prompt A/B Test
-- Compare two prompt variants using EVALUATE scoring. Pick the winner automatically.
-- Demonstrates data-driven prompt engineering built into the workflow itself.
-- Supports pre-built experiments from experiments.json or ad-hoc task + prompt_a + prompt_b.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=neural_networks
--
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=standing_desk winner_threshold=2.0
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       task="Explain neural networks" \
--       prompt_a="Explain like I'm 5" \
--       prompt_b="Give a technical explanation with analogies"
--
-- Discover available experiments:
--   spl run ... experiment_id=list

-- Scoring rubric as a reusable function
CREATE FUNCTION scoring_rubric()
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Rate the response on a scale of 0–10 for each criterion:
1. Clarity (0-10): Is it easy to understand?
2. Completeness (0-10): Does it fully address the task?
3. Relevance (0-10): Is every sentence on-topic?
4. Engagement (0-10): Is it interesting to read?

Return valid JSON only — no prose outside the JSON:
{"clarity": N, "completeness": N, "relevance": N, "engagement": N, "total": N, "rationale": "one concise sentence"}
$$;

WORKFLOW ab_test
    INPUT:  @experiment_id    TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @task             TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_a         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_b         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @winner_threshold FLOAT DEFAULT 1.5
    OUTPUT: @winner_output TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Load experiment from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Sets @experiment_context with task, prompt_a, prompt_b, and notes.
    -- If experiment_id is blank, the context will be empty and @task/@prompt_a/@prompt_b are used directly.
    CALL load_experiment(@experiment_id) INTO @experiment_context

    -- Step 2: Generate Variant A response
    GENERATE run_variant_a(
        @task,
        @prompt_a,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_a

    -- Step 3: Generate Variant B response
    GENERATE run_variant_b(
        @task,
        @prompt_b,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_b

    -- Step 4: Score both variants against the rubric (LLM)
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_a, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_a_json
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_b, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_b_json

    -- Step 5: Extract numeric totals (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_a_json) INTO @score_a
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_b_json) INTO @score_b

    -- Step 6: Pick winner or declare tie
    EVALUATE (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN > @winner_threshold THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_a
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'A',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN < (0 - @winner_threshold) THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_b
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'B',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_b - @score_a)
        OTHERWISE
            -- Scores too close — format both with explanation (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL format_tie_result(@response_a, @response_b, @score_a_json, @score_b_json) INTO @winner_output
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner  = 'tie',
                score_a = @score_a,
                score_b = @score_b
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'A/B test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

In terms of the provided explanation of neural networks with analogies, here is an updated version that incorporates some additional details and examples:

**What is a Neural Network?**

Imagine you're trying to recognize a picture of a cat. You look at the picture, but it's not immediately clear what you're looking at. That's because your brain doesn't have a clear "cat detector" that says, "Ah, I know this is a cat!"

A neural network is like a team of workers who help you figure out what's in the picture. Each worker looks at different parts of the picture and sends their findings to a central manager.

The workers are arranged in layers, and each layer processes information in a specific way. The first layer looks at simple features like edges and lines, while later layers look at more complex features like shapes and textures.

When all the workers have finished processing their part of the picture, the manager takes a final decision about what's in the picture. In this case, the manager would say, "Ah, I know this is a cat!"

This process is similar to how neural networks work in real life. The layers of neurons (the "workers") take input data and process it through complex calculations to produce an output.

**How Does a Neural Network Learn?**

Imagine you're trying to recognize different breeds of cats. You start with pictures of one breed, say Persian cats, and show them to your manager.

At first, the manager might make mistakes and say that a picture is a different breed altogether. But as you show more pictures, the manager becomes better at recognizing the features of Persian cats.

The manager doesn't just memorize what Persian cats look like; it uses the information from all the pictures to learn a pattern or rule for identifying them. This pattern or rule is called a "feature detector."

As the manager becomes more accurate at recognizing Persian cats, you can start showing it pictures of other breeds that are similar to Persians. The manager will use its learned feature detectors to recognize those breeds too.

This process is similar to how neural networks learn in real life. They're trained on large datasets of labeled information (e.g., "this picture is a cat") and use the patterns and rules they discover to make predictions or classify new data.

**Types of Neural Networks**

There are many types of neural networks, each with its own strengths and weaknesses. Here are a few examples:

* **Convolutional Neural Networks (CNNs):** These networks are great for image recognition tasks like recognizing objects in pictures.
* **Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs):** These networks are great for time-series data like speech or text, where the sequence of events matters.
* **Long Short-Term Memory (LSTM) Networks:** These networks are a type of RNN that's particularly good at handling long-term dependencies in time-series data.

Each type of neural network has its own unique architecture and training procedure. But they all share the same basic idea: using layers of neurons to process information and make predictions or classifications.

     | "Here's an example implementation in Python:
```python
import numpy as np

# Define a simple neural network with one input layer, two hidden layers, and one output layer
class NeuralNetwork:
    def __init__(self):
        self.input_layer = 784
        self.hidden_layers = [256, 128]
        self.output_layer = 10

    def forward_pass(self, inputs):
        # Forward pass through the network
        outputs = np.zeros((inputs.shape[0], self.output_layer))
        for i, hidden_size in enumerate(self.hidden_layers):
            outputs = np.tanh(np.dot(inputs, self.weights[i]) + self.biases[i])
            if i < len(self.hidden_layers) - 1:
                inputs = outputs
        return outputs

    def backward_pass(self, targets, inputs):
        # Backward pass through the network
        errors = np.zeros((targets.shape[0], self.output_layer))
        for i, hidden_size in enumerate(self.hidden_layers):
            errors = np.dot(errors, self.weights[i].T) - np.tanh(np.dot(inputs, self.weights[i]) + self.biases[i])
            if i < len(self.hidden_layers) - 1:
                inputs = outputs
        return errors

    def optimize(self, targets, inputs):
        # Optimization function for training the network
        error = self.backward_pass(targets, inputs)
        loss = np.mean(np.square(error))
        if loss < 0.01:
            print('Training complete!')
```

2. How do I explain neural networks to a beginner?

Here's an explanation of what neural networks are, including their components (layers, hidden units) and an example use case:

A neural network is a type of machine learning model that mimics the structure of the human brain. It consists of multiple layers of interconnected nodes or "neurons," which process inputs and produce outputs.

The key components of a neural network include:

* Input layer: This layer receives the input data, such as images or text.
* Hidden layers: These layers contain multiple neurons that process the input data through complex calculations. The number of hidden layers can vary depending on the specific architecture.
* Output layer: This layer produces the final output of the model.

Neural networks are trained using backpropagation, an optimization algorithm that adjusts the weights and biases of each neuron to minimize the difference between the predicted output and the actual output.

Here's an example implementation in Python:
```python
import numpy as np

# Define a simple neural network with one input layer, two hidden layers, and one output layer
class NeuralNetwork:
    def __init__(self):
        self.input_layer = 784
        self.hidden_layers = [256, 128]
        self.output_layer = 10

    def forward_pass(self, inputs):
        # Forward pass through the network
        outputs = np.zeros((inputs.shape[0], self.output_layer))
        for i, hidden_size in enumerate(self.hidden_layers):
            outputs = np.tanh(np.dot(inputs, self.weights[i]) + self.biases[i])
            if i < len(self.hidden_layers) - 1:
                inputs = outputs
        return outputs

    def backward_pass(self, targets, inputs):
        # Backward pass through the network
        errors = np.zeros((targets.shape[0], self.output_layer))
        for i, hidden_size in enumerate(self.hidden_layers):
            errors = np.dot(errors, self.weights[i].T) - np.tanh(np.dot(inputs, self.weights[i]) + self.biases[i])
            if i < len(self.hidden_layers) - 1:
                inputs = outputs
        return errors

    def optimize(self, targets, inputs):
        # Optimization function for training the network
        error = self.backward_pass(targets, inputs)
        loss = np.mean(np.square(error))
        if loss < 0.01:
            print('Training complete!')
```

        ...
